Of wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck there is no difference in treatment between it and a simple wound of the head.
But if the nerve of the neck is cut or an artery, then if it is a nerve, there should be no harm done to it except to apply something that causes strong contraction, as well as cinchona and zeki which are like vitriol and similar to them because they do harm to nerves and contract that. And don't place on the wound any cause of all heat, because the substance of nerves is heat and the continuation of it goes to the brain, which is the most important part of all the members.
And if it is healed with medicine that are light, such as washing with warm water, or by using a poultice of rosewater or not-rosewater, and then when the wound is washed with warm water and the other stones according to this way, and then anointed with oil.
But if the wound is large, then administer stitches or sew up the lips of it and then cure that with powder inside until it is healed.
But if the wound has depth or an infection in it to the lower part of pus-filled swelling, then pierce that in the lowest place.
But if in the wound an artery is cut and there is flow of blood, then separate or stop that, or ligature it, or cauterize it if necessity requires you to do so.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or all of them are broken and the veins of the neck are preserved, then sew up the lips of the wound over the cane of the neck and don't touch it; elevate that and reduce it to its natural shape and then strengthen it with decent tightness, and let it heal for several days; and then cure that with ointments in which there is contraction and excitation until it is healed.
But if the wound has been made on the bone with force, then probe it and if there are pieces of bones in it, extract all that you can; and that which to you does not appear clear, let that be inside until the wound becomes pus-filled and separate and the discharge of it be alleviated.
And if the wound is recent then powder on it the powder by which you are advised. But if a fistula is made in the wound then cure that with something of which the remembrance will come later in its chapter.
If it is a strike by a lance or a spear and you see the depth of it then consider more if from the wound there is bloating when the patient stops breathing; then know that the wound is fatal.
And if there is not depth to it and the wound is recent with its blood, then you place on it powder from the first hour and don't tighten that so that the blood isn't retained in the depth of it and return to the heart and weaken the patient, but anoint that with attractive ointment, and loose it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available to you, then place on the orifice of the wound a woolen cloth so that it sucks up what there is in humidity; and let the patient sleep on the wound so that it runs what is retained inside it.
But if from the wound three days or more have passed and there does not come to the patient spasms or trembling of the heart or tightness of the chest, and you see the other good dispositions of it, then know that the wound is healed; and then cure that with cauterization and with the other cure until it is healed.
But if its healing is prevented and it is already open for a long time then know that a fistula has already been made; cure therefore that according to its chapter.
And if the wound is by a strike of a spear or a lance and it is simple in its surface on the foot or the head then cure that with what precedes suturing if it is large or powder if it is small.
But if force has already been made on the bone and only pieces of it are broken then you twist the wound and cut to extract those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated then let them be until the wound becomes pus-filled because the extraction of them is alleviated.

Of wounds of the belly.
The disposition by which a wound occurs in the belly is either large or small or medium-sized.
And a small disruption is made difficult in another way and it is seen that its reduction is more difficult if you don't cut off the infiltration of them to their own place at once, because it opens and becomes swollen so that its insertion is made difficult.
And therefore the best disruption is medium-sized because in it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult as in the two mentioned before.
And know that there are four types of suturing of the belly, but two sutures are common and convient in suturing of the other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures are proper to each other in the suturing of the belly, but they are also proper to the suturing of the other wounds.
I will now describe to you each one with its explanation.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some cause of the intestine has exited through it, and its reduction is difficult in one of two ways: either by smallness of the disruption as we have said, or when to the intestine is given inflammation by chill of the air.
And therefore when that is so then you must scorch it with a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone or by gathering in tepid water and cyperus, and spikenard, and myrrh, and embrocation over the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it does this with that in which there is contraction and it is better than lukewarm water in resolution of the inflammation.
And therefore when you see the inflammation resolved then you moisten the intestine with water in which is steeped alder and mallows because through that the insertion of it is alleviated by a little cause.
But if the intestine is not relieved by this cure then split the disruption a little with an instrument with which its membranes are cut.
And the first cure is better when it is proper that there is no incision and no occupation to dry up afterwards and deficiency of food.
Of the instrument its form is:
One end of it is bent acute and the other end not acute, and its extremity is subtle but not with the subtlety of a spatula, but rather blunt a little.
And therefore when the wound is enlarged and you see it exiting the intestine then know that its reduction be according to its natural form in its proper place if possible because that is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and it is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you should make the patient lie on his back, and place his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should place his head and the feet highest of all.
And likewise if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then direct your intention and attention to this, so that the part which has the wound is higher than the other.
And you should administer this for large wounds and small wounds alike.
And for small wounds, apply what you have prepared, and let the minister hold it between his fingers, who should have the rupture in his hand and close its lips, and he should discover to one or another whether there is any cause for sewing.
And this is the common method of sewing: take a needle, or count of needles according to the amount of the amplitude of the wound; and leave off at the extremity of the rupture of the little finger, and tighten the needle one, then that you do not put thread in both extremes of the gut, with two extremes of the needle which is below the gut of the parts within, while you make it penetrate from this part; and you have already joined two extremes of the gut and two extremes of the needle, and so are made four turns.
And you tighten with double thread around the needle, and some times from two parts together, and you join two lips and the wound by decent gathering; and leave off at the thickness of the little finger and put another needle in that, and tie that with thread thus as is done with the first needle; and do not cease to do this if it is necessary with needles until you have completed all the sewing of the wound.
And let the needles be smaller between coarseness and thinness, for thin needles penetrate quickly into the flesh; and coarse ones are difficult to enter into the gut, and therefore let them be smaller in coarseness and thickness.
And let the needle not be pushed into two extremes of the gut next to each other, so that the flesh is not quickly pierced and that the wound opens before its consolidation.
And let the sewing not be prolonged so that the wound is prohibited from consolidation.
And you should insert the extremes of the needles so that they do not hurt the patient during his sleep; and you will place over the sewing powdered herbs from every part, which have the extremes of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is consolidated.
And these species of sewing with needles in what manner is most convenient in small wounds, for it suffices in their sewing one needle or two or around them.
But the second sewing is communal: and it is that you administer with sewing the four extremes, of the gut and two extremes of the needle, in one go, with a needle in which is thread wrapped equal in coarseness and thickness.
And when with the needle you have penetrated these four extremes, return the needle from that part from which you began, so that the thread drawn pulls the uppermost part to the wound, so that the sewing is like sewing of cloth in which the cloth is tightened.
And place between each sewing the amount of the thickness of the little finger.
And this sewing know men.
And with such sewing I closed the wound which was done to a man in his belly, which had been pierced by a knife, and was the rupture of the wound major of a palm, and had come out of the intestines around two palms from the intestine of the middle place and was the rupture in the middle of the member; and then I returned it after the intestine had exited the wound for xxiiii hours.

But the treatment of medicine for plague is different from the treatments for other illnesses.
The cause of this is that when you encounter a fresh plague wound with its blood visible and the air alters it, and you reduce the hernia and dress it decently, then apply an incorruptive powder to it.
And if the air has already altered the plague wound, then apply on it some alum in unguent form which is at your presence so that it forms a hard stone and catches the pus, and is consolidated and dried; and thus you treat this as you do with other plague wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines at your presence, then apply it immediately as soon as you notice the wound, for as long as it starts to weep, cut short hair, and change its dressing twice a day, as well as wash it with warm water, until it is healed; for in many dispositions when the plague wounds are simple, you do not need another medicine.
But if you fear that the plague will spread to the principal members, then cover wool in lion fat of good quality, or in rose oil, and apply it on the parts between the joints and below the armpits.
And if there is pain or putrefaction in his bowels, for many times this happens, then cauterize it with black styptic vinegar, especially if the putrefaction has occurred in the intestine and there is a penetrating wound within its cavity.
And know that which is of the large intestine is of a harder cure than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is of difficult cure because the intestine called "gut" does not receive cure from the plague that occurs in it at all; and this is due to the great multitude of veins that are in it, and because of its size which is in those, and because of the subtlety of that body, and because of its proximity to the nature of nerves.
But if what proceeds from the plague is the tissue, and you manage to get it while it's fresh, then reduce it according to your skill by which you reduce intestines equally.
But if it has passed into it space and has already turned green or black, then that you apply bile on the place that is blackened from it, so that blood flow comes out, for in the tissue there are veins and arteries.
And then insert what is another part of that dressing, and hang the two extremities of the bile from the edge below the plague wound, so that on you is alleviated the attraction of it because of the fastening of the tissue and emission of the pus of the plague.
Remembering of the plagues when they occur in the intestine.
When disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it might be restored in some men, thus because he drank vinegar and it was a plague wound in his belly with piercing of spear, and the plague was of the right side of the stomach, and the plague was old and made into a fistula, and there came out of it pus and wind.

And some experienced men have already said that when plague occurs in the intestine and it is small, then one should tie it according to this way: and it is that you take gum from big nuts; and then you adjust the two lips of the plague wound and place one of those gums which has an open mouth over the two lips of the plague; therefore when he takes on them and tightens his mouth, cut off the gum's stalk, for it will adhere and not dissolve.
And then place another gum near the first one, and do not cease this until you have done with gums one after another according to the quantity of the whole plague wound.
And then reduce it and close the plague wound; and let those nut stalks remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and do not let any harm occur to the patient.
And it is possible that the intestine is tied with fine thread which is made from animal intestines attached to it, since it is more in needle; and it is that you take the extremities of those intestines' threads, and let it be somewhat fleshless, and then tie the extremity of it with thread, wrapped around, and then insert that thread in needles and close with that the intestine, and reduce it in the disruption.
And this way of suture with gums is nothing but according to the way of confidence and hope.
But if the plague is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, for then there is only sublimation, and not a complete cure for it.
Of cure for ulcer and fistula.
Know that all plague and ulcer when they are closed up and become old, and there is made an ulcer, and it is not consolidated and continues to weep pus at all times and is not cut off, then in whatever member it is, universally it is called a fistula and we call it azucem.
And fistula according to truth is a knotted ulcer, hard, white with which there is no pain and to which there is no cavity as it is a cavity of bone; and therefore that is named some rizatz, that is pain.
And it is in some men moist, which lets out pus; and very much of the moisture is discharged in some hours. And sometimes this moisture is moved, and it is little, and it is thick, and it is subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs in the division, as well as the cure for them with medicines.
And thus I will make you know the cure for fistula which is made by way of operation with hand.
And know that fistula occurs in all parts of the body; for from those there are fistulas which come to large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the head, or intestine, or bladder, or any of the ribs, or to one of the joints, or to some of the vertebrae of the back, or to a similar joint; and of those there is that gives proximity to the principal member and to its likes.
And thus of these fistulas which follow this manner are of difficult healing and do not receive cure, and with them is trouble and ignorance.
And if there is any fistula whose depth does not reach these places which I have described, and you hope for and believe in its cure, and wish to attain knowledge of it, then take a probe made of iron or steel, and if the fistula goes straight ahead, probe with it.
But if the fistula is twisted, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead, for lead, because of its light weight, will be drawn and curved toward the twist.
And if the fistula has horrible movements, it is not possible for you to signify anything about it with a probe.
Then christen the orifice with holy water, for the moisture by which you cleanse it will go through the other orifices and flow from them.
And then inquire carefully, according to what manner you are able to know whether there is bone or nerve here, and whether the fistula's cavity is distant or nearby, or whether it is a fistula whose orifices are all on that spot, and according to the nature of the pain which comes with your compression of the area with your hand, and according to the significance of the causes and the swelling.
And when you have considered all these causes with true knowledge, then proceed to the cure according to your confidence: and it is that you consider whether the fistula appears nearby or in a salty place, distant from a joint, or of nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of those places which I have mentioned to you.
Then you shall dry the fistula according to what has been said before about its description, and prevent what is in that filtration and of putrefied flesh, and what is born from the orifice of flesh, or of thorns and their likes, and heal within until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of distant depth and goes straight ahead, then see that you insert it into the fistula as much as you can safely do so, and then cleanse it of all its putrefied flesh; and then administer hot waters wrapped in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula which you have understood with iron.
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotted in the cavity of the fistula's corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which produce healthy flesh within until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you shall cauterize it according to what we have recommended.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you have certified this, then you shall inquire and dry it according to what we have recommended, unless you forbid any cause of vein or nerve or major member as I have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is corruption and blackness on it, then you shall cut away that corruption until it is all gone; and then heal it with what makes flesh within until it is healed.
And if it does not heal and the ulcer comes back as before, then know that you have not attained the complete eradication of the corruption; and then discover it again and carefully find out the reason for it and cleanse it with your strongest medicine, and then restore it.
And therefore if it is healed, well and good; but if not, then know that the corruption is in the distant depth of the body, which you have not attained, and then you must only leave it alone.
And if the corrupted bone is small or you can pull it out, then you shall pull it out with subtle forceps which are suited to that purpose.
And if there are many bones, then carefully perform the extraction of all those, and leave behind none of their causes with your knowledge.
But if you encounter any cause of moisture and in their incision there is no fear or error, then insert those limbs or flesh if they are here, and deliver the bones.
But if you are not prepared to pull the bones from your patient at this time, then pause over the wound whatever will cause it to fester, and leave it so that what is around those bones of flesh and limbs, and guard the wound so that it is not consolidated but constrained, the section which you have cut in your operation.
Have you fear, pause over it a cloth dipped in oil, or a cloth dipped in Egyptian ointment, or one of the green ointments, for when those limbs fester and the bones are separated from their socket, and their attraction is driven away.
But if it is a great bone like the bone of the leg or thigh, or similar, and what is corrupted is only its membrane, then cut away that membrane behind so that the blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
But if what is corrupted is a great portion and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is a necessary cause to close that up and to cut all around where the corruption has come, and then heal that within until it is consolidated.
And I remind you to fasten one end of your patient's thigh to your bed so that he may assist you in your operation.
A young man about 30 years old had pain in his thigh, and from the parts within the jaw was matter upon the matter within the body which came to the jaw, and it is matter of ulcer with great ulcer, and there is not to him an extrinsic cause.
And then the time was prolonged by the error of the doctors until the ulcer had opened up, and matter had collected from it, and its cure was then paused until the thigh was fistulated, and there are in him many orifices through which all oozed pus and the body's moisture.
And then he was treated by many doctors for about two years, and there was not in them any relief except in the bone, where he was called.
And I saw his thigh and the matter that flowed from him through those orifices, indeed his whole body was wasted away and his color was yellow.
And then I inserted the probe into one of those orifices and reached the bone.
And then I probed all the orifices and found them coming from all parts of the thigh.
And then I cut and probed one of those orifices where I discovered a portion of the bone, and found it rotted, indeed blackened and rotted; and a portion of the thigh was already rotted where it had penetrated into the membrane.
And then I seared that which had been discovered and prepared from the exposed bone.
And I found that it was not in the bone but only that corruption which I had cut off and seared, and I had already removed it.
And then I paused over the wound' restoration with consolidative medicine for a long time, but it was not consolidated.
And then I probed and discovered the bone again a second time over the first discovery, and found continued corruption in the bone.
And I seared what appeared to me from that corruption, and then I found it necessary to perform the cure of it and its restoration, but it was not restored.
And then I uncovered him again and did not cease from cutting into the small bones little by little particularly and piercing through to the bone, and it was not closed up until I had cut around a palm's width of him, and that [bone] I extracted with its marrow, and then I restored that one with medicines, and it was consolidated quickly and healed.
And it is not necessary to repeat the operation on him or to cut into him again except by the disposition of his sickness and because of his lack of tolerance for it and fear of death, since at all times he fainted from the excess of evacuation.
And so he is healed by complete healing, and there is nothing in the place of hard bone; and his disposition has become good in his body and his strength has returned; and he is restored to his usual dispositions, and he has no difficulty walking or harm in any way.
But if there is bone protruding from the body, then you should proceed in this manner: Take some ligature and tie it tightly around the end of the protruding bone; have someone else pull on it upwards while you hold it down with a piece of woolen cloth which is thicker than the first one; and then tie that over the meat below the bone and stretch out the end of it so as to draw the flesh away from the part below, and uncover the flesh at the place where you want to cut so that the saw does not cut into the flesh.
And put a board or pillow under the bone properly so that when you do this it is not forbidden for the saw to cut into the rotten flesh.
And it is necessary that the saw be used on the rotten place little by little, because if the corruption is in the hollow of the bone and does not appear in its appearance to sensation, then it will force another cut elsewhere.
And if the bone is rotten and not protruding, then it is one part continued from the other, and the corruption is in its middle or in a part of it; then uncover all parts completely; and put a board under it, and cut there in the first part where the corruption is until the cutting continues to the other part.
And the sawing should be according to the location of the corruption a little bit at a time as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut into that corrupted joint and saw through the bone where it continues from the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is not a reason for that except foolishness.
And if the corruption is in the knuckle of the hand or foot it is very difficult; however, see what the corruption appears to you like and open it up, and treat it according to whatever way that seems possible to you, and with every foolishness be corrected by you when there is no contrariness caused by a vein or nerve.
And you should know that there are many ways of cutting and the saws for cutting these bones depend on the situation of the bones and their preparation, and thickness, thinness, magnitude, smallness, hardness, rarity, and so forth; therefore, when you prepare any kind of instrument for an operation, be careful to fit it to that operation.
And you should know that this operation tells you about the kind of instrument of which you are in need; when you have great study and long experience of the ways of this art, and knowledge of the ways of diseases, for then you already have through your own experience what is convenient to instruments for all illnesses.

Of cutting edges and piercing through bones.
The edges may become blunt either by an intrinsic cause or by an extrinsic one, or when you have treated that corruption with those medicines and the healing does not correspond, or sometimes the corruption goes away from the part of the body in which it is not caused by any cut, then you should cut that part of the body where the corruption is located, so that the patient may escape death or a greater harm to that member.
And the sign of him to whom it happens is that the member turns black while it is being healed as if fire had burned it, or it becomes black afterwards when that burning has gone into that member which feels it and which affects the whole body; therefore, be careful about cutting that.
And similarly if the cause of the corruption is the percussion of some poisonous things such as scorpion venom, or snake venom, or adder's venom, or the like.
And if the corruption or the percussion is in the end of the finger, then cut the finger and do not give space to the corruption so that it goes into the hand.
And similarly if it comes to the hand, then cut that from the knuckle of the fire-bow, and do not give space to the corruption so that it goes into the bow of the arm.
And if instead of that you cut the arm at the elbow in the joint.

And similarly cut the foot when corruption comes to the toe of the foot, then cut that from one of the toes.
And if it takes in the sole of the foot, then cut all the foot. But if it goes into the paw, then cut the leg at the joint of the paw.
And if the corruption has already come upon the paw then there is no remedy except release, and let the patient go to death.
And the way of cutting the member or of sawing it is that you tighten the ligature in the part which you want to cut, and tighten another ligature over the place; and extend the minister one ligature to the lower parts, and extend the other minister ligature above; and separate the flesh which is between two ligatures then split it lengthwise so that all the flesh be uncovered; and then let it be cut or sawn.
And have cloth placed over him from all sides so that it does not touch the affected area, and let the patient be given a poultice of heated lead and a cautery.
If there is bleeding, then cauterize the wound or apply some powders that restrict the blood flow; then return to your treatment until it is complete.
Then bind the affected limb with a bandage in a comfortable manner and treat it until it is healed.
I have seen a man whose foot was affected in this way, as described by you; the cause was blackness in his foot from burning, resembling fire burns.
And that corruption began first in his toe, then spread throughout the entire foot.
And then that man prepared himself remedies so that the treatment would go to the affected limb with his great desire and effort; he applied them until the joint was healed and the pain and burning ceased.
For a long time, that man had suffered from that corruption in the toe of his right hand.
And then he came to me, and I removed that excess with the medicines I applied to his body; thus, the purification of his body was accomplished, and the excess did not spread to the other toe but began in the hand where it had started.
He begged me to cut off his hand, but I refused, fearing the spread of that excess and more so because of the risk of the hand becoming gangrenous due to the seriousness of the condition.
And when he despaired of me, he returned to his homeland and found that man who prepared and cut off his entire hand, and it healed.
And I have not added this narrative except that it may be an aid to those who suffer from this disease and as a sign by which it is recognized and treated accordingly.
On the treatment of wounds and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy part and the space of the abscess is prolonged so that it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus is discharged, and the wound remains hollow as if it were a vessel, and the cord-like tissue above it is removed with a thin cloth, and there is no further corruption, nor does it affect the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinuous wound and not a fistula.
However, if the space is prolonged so that corruption spreads to some part, then it is called a fistula and not a sinuous wound.
And its treatment is that the cord-like tissue is entirely removed as it is rolled up, especially when it is thinned down and treated with a cloth, and you must be careful not to cause adhesion to the affected area due to the corruption.
But if you expect the cord-like tissue to adhere and the corruption has not yet caused any damage, and it has extended forward, then you should treat it with enemas.
And you should consider the sinuous wound; if it is large and the flesh gives off a fetid odor, then treat it with an Egyptian medicine which is made of oil, wine vinegar, and honey, mixed together in equal parts and placed in a vessel over fire until it becomes thick like honey.
Then take what is necessary from it and dissolve it in water and honey; treat the wound with this, close its opening, and leave the medicine in for two hours.
Then remove it with care; do this for several days until the wound is purified and healed.
And sometimes it is enema with water of ashes when you do not have the medicine available; take ash from a vitis vine and put water on it, then stir it up and treat the wound with this until you are sure that the wound is healed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then treat it with water and honey in which is mixed ground zingiber, and also with wine and honey mixed, for the property of honey is to purify and cleanse, and wine adheres to the wound, especially when there is an excess of dryness and astringency in the wine.
And when you do this several times and you are sure that the wound is healed, then treat it with something that makes flesh grow in it.
And take some thin myrrh and dissolve it in rose oil and astringent wine; or treat the wound with other medicines and ointments that I have written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow and the enema cannot be inserted, then widen it gradually with a small iron rod and place a poultice wrapped in an Egyptian ointment or a basilicon ointment over it until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring the edges together with stitches and leave a small amount inside so that the enema can be inserted into it.
And if the opening through which the arm extends upward is elevated above the surrounding parts, then dry that part in the pus which is below it so that the arm can extend downward; for when the arm is contract in a sinuous wound, flesh is generated in it.
And if you cannot dry the wound in the bone, then you must have the limb prepared so that the arm extends outward freely according to your estimation, and there must be no cause for the arm to be retained in it.
But that which is over the opening of the wound, let it be kept in place for as long as it takes to heal what is bound in the arm. And so that you may know whether what runs out of it is blood or pus, or undigested food, or digested:
And according to this you consider the location of that wound, whether it causes pain in the patient or not, and if there is an abscess in it:
And when your treatment is prolonged in this manner, and you see the matter that has been applied to the opening of the wound already adhere, or it has acquired adhesion, then you should loosen it on the third day, and when you remove the dressing, then change the ointment.
But if from the wound a subtle pus extends downward to a great distance, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is proper; for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh occurs slowly in the wounds, and others in whom it occurs quickly.
And if from the wound an indigestible arm extends for several days, then know that the wound has not adhered; and if the adhesion of the wound is delayed and its cause prolonged, then adjust your medicines in desiccation, and if your medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, then dry them with myrrh ointment, when there is an excess of astringency in it.
And the most effective medicine for this condition is to take a thin ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and apply it on the affected area, and use it often.
And if there are no bandages available, take thin ointment, honey when it is cut and thickened, and use it; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind them all into powder over the fire, and spread this powder on a cloth and place it over the wound; and spread the ointment that you have cut and thickened over the honey, and spread it on a cloth.
And sometimes you should administer to it long-stemmed aristology and roots of celestial lily (and celestial lily) and flour of oats and centaurea, and use these simples or prepare them, grind them up and sieve them into powder over the honey that I have mentioned; and use this.
And if the wound is prolonged and not healed by this treatment, then understand that the corruption is in the flesh of it, and make an inquiry into the bones or other hard members or to the tendons; then treat it with a fistula treatment, as has been said above.
On the treatment of nail fungus and nail scraping and addition of digit incision and section.
Panarici is an apostema which arises below the nail of the toe or finger, and very rarely in other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its cure is less valued, or it is apoplectic of apostema cauterized, or it is corrupted, and causes a pus under that corrodes the root of the nail, and very often corrupts all of it, and very often its corruption pervades to the bone within which has an offensive odor, and the extremity of the finger becomes lame, and its color becomes dark.
And therefore when you have this with what we have said in the division and do not bring about your cure, then understand that you should pour out all the superfluity that is rotted from the nail and then cauterize the wound properly, because cautery is beneficial in this case.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is healed, and the edges of the nail start to press into the flesh between the fingers, and begin to sting and irritate it, then understand that you should place a fine needle under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and drive it upwards and cut that flesh with ease, and place over it something that produces burning corrosion while it is still moist; and then cure this with an ointment until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already penetrated to the bone, then understand that you should cut out the bone and extract it, because the bone which is corrupted will never be healed.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption spreading to the finger, then cut it at one of the joints according to where the memory is, and then cure this until it is healed.
But if the nail has a concussion or abrasion, and subsequently it causes great pain in that place, then at the beginning you should bleed the patient and then dry the nail with a sharp spatula with a transverse cut above it, and be careful that the cut does not penetrate into the flesh which is below the nail, because you will cause great pain to the patient, and this would result in the birth of superficial flesh in that location; and then cure that place until the pain subsides.
But a finger which is attached to the hand of some men, is very rarely all flesh, or it has no bones in it, or it is only a nail.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or their origin is in some of the fingers. But he who is born on some fingers does not suffer harm, and he who is born at the joints suffers much harm.
And for those which are born fleshy, the cut is easier, and you should cut it below its root with a broad spatula.
But for he who is born at the root of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of the falling off of it.
But for he who is born on the finger between the joints, understand that you should at the beginning cut into the flesh of it with a round incision into the bone, and then saw it with one of those convenient saws for this, and then cure this until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between them very often or due to the nature of man who is born that way, or due to consolidation of pus or by fire, or similarly, therefore understand that you should dry up that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them lint soaked in rose oil so that they are not consolidated easily, and separate these from one another, or place between them a thin sheet of lead according to where it is consolidated.
And similarly if consolidation happens in some fingers with the palm, then dry up that consolidation according to how it is prepared and convenient for the figure of the member.
Of cuts from time to time, these are varicose veins.
Varicose veins and varicose veins, are large twisted veins full of melancholy humors which appear in the parts of the body, and their greatest occurrence in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and carriers.
Therefore understand that you should administer a purgative to the body of black cholera several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But their cure by cupping is according to two manners: the one is that it is cut and that black blood is drawn, and the other manner is that the vein is elevated and all of it is extracted.
Its cut is according to this manner: at the beginning soak the leg with warm water until the gross blood which is foul from the leg is resolved, and then bind up the leg of the patient at the place above the pus which is near to the part below the knee with a bandage, and then dry the vein in one place, or two places, or three places with a broad cut, and then elevate the black blood by the sole of the foot upwards to within it, and let the amount of blood which you desire and can endure be expelled, and the strength of the patient bear it.
And then ligature that, and command him that he abstain from foods generating black cholera.
And repeat the cure by cupping when the veins are full and this afflicts the patient.
But the elevation of them is according to this manner: you who raise the leg of the patient if it pains him, and then place him in a bath and soak the leg with warm water until he becomes warm and the veins are swollen; or that he is exercised by strong exercise, if bathing is not at hand, until the member is warmed.
And then dry up the vein on the outside of the leg by a cut in the thigh or at the back of it below the knee, or in the sole of the foot below the toe.
And then open up the vein with tweezers and scrape the vein in every part where it appears to the sense, and it is due to the appearance that it inclines towards blackness.
And therefore when you see this and the vein, you will see it white and like corded.
And then you should place below that the rayon so that it is elevated and gitted from the vein, and support that with a lion's tooth.
And then cut another incision near that incision to a depth of three fingers, and then excise the vein that appears above it.
And then elevate that and incise it behind the second incision down to the bone, and then extract that and elevate what is within from the second incision, and then extract that from above the incision.
And then do what is necessary to extract it from the incision that is highest or uppermost of all the incisions, so that everything is exposed; and then incise it.
And if it does not yield to you when you try to extract and elevate it, then insert a strong needle with a thread through it and ligature it, or insert it below the bone and move your hand over all parts where it is lodged. And be careful not to incise it; for if it is broken, it is difficult for you to elevate that part and it has come upon the patient through that harm.
And when all of that has been completely expelled, then place over the site of the ulcer wool saturated in wine and rose oil, and cure that which is healed.
And if the vein is contracted and twisted with an enlargement at its parts, and it does not follow rectitude according as we have said, then you should split that throughout all of its twists and the locations of its enlargement, and then suspend that with hooks so that you elevate it all.
And be careful not to incise the vein and not to harm it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your discretion in that.
Form of the incision with which the vein is dried:
It is not accuteed with the hooks or acute at its end so that it does not harm the vein; and it has great flexibility, for if it were subtle, it would incise the vein because of its subtlety; there is superfluous thickness in it as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is engendered in the legs in lower lands like Al-Ategi in the arid and barren lands of Arabia and in the lands of India that are lower and arid with little tenderness, and in which there is not grass nor herbage.
And very rarely is it engendered in other locations of the body other than the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes beneath the membrane like venom within the body of serpents and worms and snails and worms engendered between the membrane and the flesh.
And a sign of the occurrence of it is that this vein happens in inflammation and swelling in the leg, and then the location becomes vesicated; and then the vein begins to emerge from the location of that vesication looking like a root of a plant.
And then when you see the end of it, you must wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one drachma and a half, and keep it in place and let the lead hang from the leg.
And as many times as it causes some pain at its parts going outward, wrap that with lead and hold it. And if the pain is prolonged, then you incise a part of it and wrap the remaining part; and do not incise that which is rooted so that it be exposed, for if you incise that, it will contract and enter into the flesh and cause an apostema and swelling at the location and a bad ulcer.
For it should be treated gradually and extracted little by little so that it is expelled from its end and does not cause any harm in the body.
And from this vein some people are affected who are in their length of five or six palms and I have seen one who was twenty palms.
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then insert the bone into its hollow and perforate it with a long hole through to the body so that everything that is in the matter is evacuated, and then administer putrefaction of the location for several days with medicines; and then cure that with the cure of apostemas.
And this vein sometimes has ram-like pains, and mostly when it appears at the joint of the leg or in the leg, and it comes to it pains from horrible places, and is engendered from every orifice of a ram.
And therefore cure that as we have said in the division and with what is above.
Of the section of worms engendered under the skin and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is named in some land because of its boun illness due to worms which are engendered between the membrane and the flesh, and go throughout the body pushing and descending, and are revealed to the sense by the movement of a member from member so that they rupture the membrane and emerge out.
And its generation is from putrefaction of certain humors like the engendering of worms and serpents and roundworms and tapeworms in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it goes in the body and is elevated to the head, and it has reached the throat, then very rarely does it open in it and enter and destroy the throat, and that happens sometimes.
And therefore when you want to cure that and extract it, it is not done unless through the movement of it and its appearance to the sense, and then you should tighten what is over it and what is under it with a ligature and compression very strongly, and then cut over it and extract that.
And if it is deep in the flesh and not approaching, then you place cautery with fire over the location where it resides, and the greatest cause that is feared is corruption of it by which it corrupts the location as we have said.
And if you want it to be brought to the head near the throat, then tighten the neck strongly with a tight ligature, and then cut over it, and extract that; and let the patient use purification of his body with evacuant medicines, and guard against food engendering putrefaction.
Of the section on infant illness.
This illness is named in our land infant, and it is a pain which comes in some members, and then it is transferred from member to member.
And I will tell you about that according as he recounted to me. He was called to a sick woman in a village, and he saw her arm and noticed a small inflammation in the vein of the arm, and after it was swollen for an hour, he saw that inflammation go up as though it were a living worm, but more quickly than if it were silver when it goes from place to place.
And then it went to the pain of that location and settled in the muscle, and then it stayed there for an hour.
And then it went through the rest of the body until it reached the other arm.
And then he recounted to me that it moved around through the body, second as he saw it; and it was too marvelous about its transformation and the speed of member to member. And he had seen before that in the same way, but he had seen pains which are transferred from member to member. But I have not seen that with the arms as with the woman.
And I did not think that was because the woman was old, being of sixty years and with veins exposed, and in her appeared to the sense that twitching transformed.
And it is necessary that this retro-cure not appear in those who are delicate, with humid bodies and veins hidden.
And when you want to cure him and the patient feels that pain, then it is not for you to appear like that as we have said, then you stretch him out and reduce him lightly and dry him on the spot from which that windiness has come out, and cauterize the site.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then you should treat him with a purging of the body and with that which expels windiness, and those carmine pills with foul-smelling pills and serapium pills and similar ones.
On the extraction of arrows:
Arrows are diverse according to their species, and according to the parts of the body in which they strike, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small ones, and concave, and blunt ones, and some are solid; and among them are those that have three angles, and others that have tongues.
And the signs of the soul are when an arrow strikes it, and penetrates the bone, and exits through the foramen which is above the brain, for then occur violent soda, and scotomata, and vertigo, and heat in the eyes, and inflammation and heat of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of the mind, and vomiting of bile, and the voice is greatly impaired and destroyed, and moisture exits from the site of the wound white like flour, and his eyes resemble water.
And so if these signs appear to you, then you should abstain from cure and extraction of the arrow unless it is stuck outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it strikes the heart are that it is near the left nipple, and feels like it has been stuck in a hard substance, and is not a hollow cause; and quickly the movement of the arrow is like the movement of the pulse, and flows from the wound black blood, and descend all ten parts.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are effusion of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are swollen, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes in a great spirit, and demands frequent breathing.
And if the arrow strikes the diaphragm which is in the chest, then the pain is near the little ribs, and a great wound is made with violent pain and a sigh, and all the limbs of the muscles are not weakened.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then quickly see the wound with some digested food.
And if the arrow strikes the belly and is lodged in it, see any slit of the wound, or the fabric, or intestine which has been torn, and there is no hope for cure by extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder and exits urine, and processes some cause to the parts outside, and is great pain on the patient, then know that it is deadly.
But the other members such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders and their like of the limbs, are saved according to the most prominent dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery, and is not a venomous arrow.
And I will recount to you what I have seen of these arrows, so that you may understand through this about your cure.
The cause is, because in one man an arrow struck the lacrimal bone of his eye on the root of his nose, and he extracted it from the other side, leaving the bones of his eyebrow, and he was healed, and did not occur in his eye any harm.
And extracted another arrow from a Jew which he struck in him on the bones of his eye, below the eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden, and he did not obtain it except for a little end with which it adhered to his staff, and it was a great arrow of the pointed arrows of the Turks, with lion ferrule, in which there were no eyebrows.
And the Jew was healed, and no harm occurred to his eye badly.
And extracted another arrow from a Christian man which had struck him in the throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and it was that to which the eyebrows belong; and then I inserted it between two fingers, and it was already submerged in his throat, and I made it thin with that, and then extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And extracted another arrow from a man which had struck him in the belly, and I considered that he would die from it; and therefore, after they had passed thirty days and no change occurred to him of his dispositions, I inserted into the arrow and probed it, and he was healed, and no bad accident occurred to him.
And I saw a man in whom an arrow struck his back, and the wound was consolidated on him, and after fire close to seven years, the arrow exited through the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman in whom an arrow struck her belly, and the wound was consolidated within her, and she did not change in her dispositions for ten days, and the woman did not suffer harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow struck his face and the wound was consolidated, and the arrow was broken, not causing here any pain, and similar movements.
And extracted the arrow of the king's prince who was struck in the middle of his nose, and he had already declined to the right party a little, and the arrow was all hidden.
And I was called to his cure close to the extraction of the arrow after three days.
And then I probed the wound of the arrow with a slender probe, and he did not feel it; but he felt a puncture below the eyebrow on the right, and then I perceived that this puncture was from the end of the arrow.
And I applied the place with ointment in which is verity atractive and digestive, believing from me that the place would be exposed so that it appears to me the location of the arrow, drying on it.
And no significant accident occurred that the arrow had achieved that location; and I continued with the ointment on it for several days, and no accident occurred; and the wound between time was consolidated, and he healed the exit of his nose by space of several days after which one day he felt the arrow in his nose and recognized it to me.
And then I paused on the wound acute corrosive medicine for several days until fire exited, and with the probe I probed that one, and I found the end of the arrow which adhered to the staff.
And then I saw the plague disappear with that sharp medicine, and after some time, about four months, it completely went away.
And when the plague became inflamed, and I was able to insert forceps into it without causing any discomfort, then I stopped trying to remove it with my hands using various techniques, and instead began using forceps to extract it gently, as you will see from the description of these techniques in the following chapter. And so I removed this plague.
And some doctors believed that the cartilage of the nose could not be restored, but I was able to restore it, and the plague was healed, and the patient recovered completely and experienced no relapses.
And I will now describe to you the process of extracting certain arrows, so that you may understand the rules and significance of what I have not described, for the parts of this craft and their definitions are not fully explained in this book.
And wise artisans compare a few things to many, and what is present to what is absent, and they discover new operations and new instruments as a result of the occurrence of foreign causes in their craft.
And so I say that arrows are not removed from the parts in which they are lodged, but according to two methods: either by extracting the part in which they are stuck, or by using the opposite method of the other part.
And an arrow that is stuck in a fleshy part is extracted by being pulled out from where it entered, or it is removed and extracted.
And if it does not come out immediately, then you should leave it for a few days until the flesh around it has healed and the inflammation and pain have subsided.
Similarly, if it is stuck in a bone and does not come out immediately, then leave it for a few days and repeat the extraction and movement every day until it comes out.
And if it does not come out after a few days, then you should drill a hole through the shaft of the arrow in that bone, from every angle, until the hole is enlarged and then extract the arrow.
But if the arrow is deeply lodged in the body and hidden from sight, then you should probe it with your fingers and, if you feel it, pull it out using some tools that are suitable for extracting it.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the plague and the length of the arrow, and there is no bone or nerve or vein in the way, then cut through the plague until it is enlarged enough for you to extract the arrow.
And if there are nails involved and they are stuck, then you should loosen the flesh all around where the arrow is lodged using every technique you can think of; and devise a way to do this, if you cannot remove the arrow through the opening in the flesh created by the fracture of the nails or by twisting them.
And when you are extracting the arrow from any part, use your fingers with forceps in every way possible to make it yield; and use a very gentle touch so that the arrow is not broken and its extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot extract it immediately, then leave it for a few days until the parts around it have healed, and then try again as it will then be easier to remove. But if you are faced with a flow of blood, then follow the instructions for treatment that we have given in that chapter.
And be careful not to cut any veins or nerves, or pierce the bowels, and proceed with every possible precaution to extract the arrow; do this slowly and carefully, just as we have recommended to you.
And therefore, you should contract the arrow so that the patient is in the same position as when the arrow struck him, for this is more beneficial. But if this is not possible for you, then do what you can according to the figures.
But the arrows that are stuck in the opposite part, or that have already entered some part on the outside, or cause a pulsing sensation in the part above the cordian vein, then tighten the tip of the arrow by feeling the affected part with your fingers; then cut it off at the place where the forceps are holding it; and then pull it out, for it is easier to extract in this way.
But if it is stuck in the bone, then twist the arrow according to its opposite angle until the arrow cuts into the bone and widens the opening; then extract it.
But if not, leave it for a few days and then try again until you succeed in extracting it.
And if the shaft of the arrow is in that part, then push it through with your fingers.
But if the shaft is broken and you want to administer pressure, then insert your finger into the hole of the arrow so that it fits into the hollow of the shaft and then push it with your finger.
But if the arrow is hollow, then push it with a strong instrument inserted into it, for the arrow will be pushed out by this.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then hollow out all the flesh around where it is lodged, if you can do so, and then heal that wound with whatever you think is appropriate.
And if the arrow hits the foot, or the belly, or the bladder, or the side, and it is near enough for you to feel with your fingers, then probe it and be careful not to cut any veins or nerves, extract it and then heal the wound if there is bleeding.
The shape of the forceps used to extract arrows:
Have their ends shaped like the beak of an owl, which grip the arrow so that it does not slip out.
And they are made in various shapes, large, small, medium or thin, according to the size of the arrow and its thickness, and the width of the wound and its tightness.

Oh son, learn that the foolish doctors and the vulgar and those who have never read the books of the Ancients nor understood a letter of them boast about this chapter.
And for this reason this manner of the wise men in our land is hidden; and I have not been obliged to do so at any time until now; and I have not used what I have learned, except through long study of the books of the Ancients and contemplation upon their wisdom, so that I might extract knowledge from them.

Summary and collections from being fractured bones, from which their premise is necessary before we begin to remind the members of the fracturing and various of each. And therefore I say that when it happens to someone fracture or dislocation or twist or hernia, then you should first apply phlebotomy or bleeding of that, or any embrocation, if no counterindication, such as debility of strength, or the person is an infant, or elderly in decrepitude, or it is a time of violent heat, or violent friction.
And let him be fed on a diet of fresh herbs and meats of birds and crabs; and abstain from fattening foods and from all foods that fill the veins with blood, so that you ensure the formation of a firm scab, and if you do not fear the effusion of matter into the limb.
And then return the patient to his usual diet with which he is accustomed.
And therefore when the broken bone begins to be restored, then let the patient be fed on nutritious foods of light and heavy foods in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked in water, rice, capers, fish, cow's belly, eggs, fresh peas, wine, and heavy foods similar to these; and with this diet coagulation of the fracture is made more quickly and gently.
And know that the bones are broken when they are in the first youth and in the elderly, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their nature due to the withering of their limbs.
However, nature adjusts and connects the broken bone of every part any cause similar to glue in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens together, so that it comes to an end of strength and firmness, as fire at the beginning, so that no cause prohibits it from its works.
And for this reason, it is necessary to stop the patient's foods, foods in which there is weight and viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is different from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is different from the fracture of the bone of the toe; and likewise all the other limbs are diverse among themselves.
And I have experienced the reminder of every kind of fracture, explained in its distinct chapter from the others.
Great diversity of fracture within itself. Great sign of fracture of the bone. And by that is known fracture of the bone: its crookedness and its protuberance and appearance to the touch, and also when you press it with your hand.
And therefore when there is not in the location apparent crookedness nor is it made due to your touch when you press the bone with agitation and the patient does not complain of great pain, then it is not here fracture.

Method of plaster which works for wounds and pain: take powdered myrrh, cedar wood, human hair or owl feathers, horehound, and salt, all in equal parts; pound them together, sift and mix to make a plaster.

And if none of the causes that we have mentioned happen to the member, then do not remove it but rather leave it for three or four or five or seven days; and some times it is left for twenty days according to how it appears to you from the appearance of the affected member, as we have said, until the fracture is healed and the meat closes over that.
And then adjust the compression according to how it was before in your first compression, and adjust the thickness of the bandage on the patient's body, according to how his memory precedes it.
