And I have not seen any of them do that, for it is more reasonable that they do not do so.
Chapter according to: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and the figures of them are diverse, and the causes are distinct; for some fractures are caused by blow of a staff, and the blow pushes all the bone inside the head, which is below the skull, just as it pushes in the trunk, and because of that this species of fracture is called compression; or the staff penetrates one part of the bone, and penetrates the flesh around it, and the penetration does not go through the end of it; and this species of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the wound of these two fractures is large or small, and the fracture which is compression or crushing; and the cause of it is blow with fists or clubs on stone, and to them similar.
And this fracture is penetrating so that it approaches the membrane which is below the bone; or it is in the bone's face, and the wound of these two fractures is large or small.
And of fracture which is dulled in the bone, in the thickness of the hair, and because of that this species of fracture is called capillary fracture.
And of fracture which is caused by blow or penetration of stone to them similar, and makes some lamina of the bone enter into the gaps between the teeth, and is caused in the concave place just as it happens to the fingers when a blow is struck and is split into the gaps between the fingers.
And this happens mostly in bodies of fat people, as well as children's heads.
And there are all these species of fractures, they are like broken stars. And then you will have the memory of everything in its place.
And know all these species of fractures by examination on these and by inquiry with their fingers and appearance of the corrupted flesh that is on them.
And hairline fracture is known by what is uncovered over the bone, and it is wiped away, and liniment is applied over it, because the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of fractures consider at the beginning the accidents of the patient, and then if you see through the accidents that indicate by obvious indication fear, such as vomiting of cholera, and extension, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and sweating of those, and appearance to them of the accidents in the greater dispositions, six days are not healed.
And if you see no-running accidents, then you expect health, then you begin the cure of it.
The reason for this is that if the victim has come to you in the beginning as he was victim and that is in the days of winter, then you should study in the appearance of the bone before day fourteen according to all disposition.
And if in the days of summer, then you should cut the bone before the seventh day, having that what is under the skin be corrupted from the padding, and that the accidents we have counted happen.
And if the fracture has already reached the padding covering the brain, and it is in minute pieces and crushed, then you should insert the crushed and ground up piece according to what I will recount to you.
And it is that you shave the head of the victim who has been victimized, and uncover over the bone according to habit and manner possible to you according to the figure of the plague, and that be lighter on the victim.
And if you have to do the detection of the bone, blood flow or pus cauterize, then you should oppose it with what covers, and it is that you dress the place of drapes infused in wine and rose oil, and let the plague rest while the pustule is settled, and be certain of the blood flow; and then begin in the circuit of the bone and in its removal.
And that be according to one of two manners of operation.
And the one manner is that you insert the bone with a narrow incision knife, and this is its form: And then administer another incision knife this, a little wider than that; and this is its form:
And then administer the incision knife wider than the second.
And it should be that you have many incision knives, of which some are wider than others; and some are shorter than the others and are in the thickness of their sharpness, and are of iron sharpened or filed.
And use them with facility in percussion over the incision knife so that it does not rattle the head and lead that one.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then you should bore in the circuit of it before you administer the incision knives with the drills, which are named non-deep drills.
And they are not named so but because those do not stop the end of the bone to it that is aprop it, so that to the drill is round extremities or margin over that which is under the head of it sharp resembling a margin or small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills great number of which each suits the thickness of each bone, while being present in all bones of the brain the extremities of which are acute, the quantity in length and brevity is according to the quantity of thickness of this test.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of drilling in the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the drill over the bone and rotate it with your fingers while you know that the bone has been penetrated; and then change the drill to another location, and pause what is between all the way the amount of thickness of the ray or environment that.
And then you insert with incision knives what is between each crack of the bone, and make that more subtle than you can over that at end of elevation, as we have said, within erradiques the bone, or with your hand, or with any other cause by some tools which you will have equipped as pins and forceps subtle.
And it should be that you keep with great care that the incision knife does not touch any cause of the brain.
And then when you have bored the bone perforated, and separated from the brain if it is continued with it, and have that extracted, then shave and bite that in the roughness of it from what is in the bone with another instrument similar to an incision knife, but however let it be thicker and more subtle than the other incision knives.
And if any part of the small and broken bones, then take that with facility with what it is due to you of the instruments; and then that cure the plague with poultices and ointments which we have said.
And the other manner of operation is gentle, distant from fear; and it is called Gualia and is called by great laziness; and that is the name of it: it is necessary for you in the beginning to begin to uncover the part of the bone of the place where is the fracture most violent and most manifest, and then when you have uncovered that part and have paused below it the end of this incision knife long and gentle of which this is its form:
And the lenticular part is round and does not insert any cause, and the acute part is of it in two sides in length, as you see; that the lenticular part be fitted to the brain with the facet of it inscribing acute in the bone.
And then you pierce on the incision knife of one part with a small needle within that you insert all the bone with facility as it is rotated, and be certain of the padding that no accident happen to you in any manner which you fear in any way, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he was sleeping.
Then if it penetrates any cause attached to the bone of the padding in some locations of bone, pierce that from it with the end of the incision knife lenticular, and loosen that from it with facility, because it is loosened from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate within the padding, and what is severed or is left of the bone is the facet of it, and you have in it roughness and minute cracks, then shave that roughness and loosen those cracks with subtle rasors by which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that be to you possible to administer them in all locations the most convenient of them and the most direct of those according to what in which you are involved your operation that broken bone and the form of it.
And it should be that you administer in your reason by which you shave the bone at the beginning the wider of those rasors, and then use after it the most subtle of them, and do not cease doing that according to continuity until you arrive to administer the most thick and the most subtle of all those.
But in each of the other small and fine fractures and subtle fracture, let you administer cure according to what you have arrived at rectification of it.
And it is caused by not being removed from all those who have studied in that art. And as for this, what we write about, we have shown in great fractures.
And if the skin is stretched when you remove the bone, then take a piece of cloth according to the amount of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and place that on top of the opening of the wound; and then take another piece of folded or doubled cloth, soak it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first piece of cloth, and do this lightly with pus on that, so that you don't press hard on the head. And then apply salt water to it, and do not tighten it except for the amount that the wound is, only.
And let him remain with this treatment for one or two days until you are sure of cauterization; and then loosen that, and administer medicines that get rid of superfluous moisture, like flax and linseed flour, and powder of fennel and of aristology, and of similar things to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that is sprinkled over the wound, as they are dry; and generally, administer in that all medicine of the property of which is that cleanses and does not bite.
And be careful according to your virtue that the wound in your treatment is clean; and let there not be in it any filth of ointment or other; nor let you allow virulence to accumulate on it; for when it is applied to the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and destroys it, and malicious evil occurs over the patient accordingly.
And accidents occur in the head, as the bone is removed from it and especially when the treatment of it is diminished, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore consider if the accidents that we have said occur to the patient, know that they are fatal without a doubt.
And if blackness does not occur but from the medicine that is placed on it, and it is due to that medicine that causes that blackness, then take honey in one part and rose oil in three parts. And soak a piece of cloth with those, and then place that on the head; and then cure that with the spices of treatment that are convenient until it is healed.
Chapter .iii.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts, both above, or one of those some few times, because in it there are two bones, and the lower part is cartilage, and is not broken, and nothing occurs to it except curvature and twisting and sinusitis.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you must put your thumb in his nose and close that fracture with your index finger and your thumb, so that the nose returns according to its natural shape. And let it be done by you with facility, and be careful that it does not hurt the patient through that operation.
And if the fracture is in the highest part of the nose and the finger does not reach it, then let it be split with a sharp edge at its tip to make it a little thicker.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same thing.
And try to restore it in the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then you must allow the cautery wound to heal for two or three days.
And then insert at the end of the nose a plug of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or insert two plugs, if the fracture is in two parts, and let there be in the plug thickness of such quantity that it fills the crack in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorators say that the plugs are wrapped in fat, and are changed every day; but I have not seen it; I had rather that the plug be wrapped in yolk of egg with powdered sugar, and let the bone be bound and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes let a plug be inserted in the nose instead of a plug, which is a goose feather wrapped in linen cloth; and let the fracture of the nose be very strong, and let the patient not be prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want to do it, then do it, and if you want to do plugs.
And if another cautery wound occurs, plaster the nose with a poultice or with cotton soaked in wine and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if a cautery wound does not occur, then plaster that outward with sesame flour and fennel powder mixed with yolk of egg; and then place on it a plug of linen cloth, and do not tie the nose for any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then split them and extract them with instruments that are appropriate for those; and then sew the wound, and cure it with something that causes flesh to grow and harden, and with plasters that are convenient for that.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then cure that with plugs and administer lead tubes so that it is completely healed.
Chapter .iiii.
On the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and its fracture is not with pus, then be careful if the fracture is outward only and is not split in two and curved inwards, then its cognition is obscure.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then insert your left index finger in the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left side, then insert your right index finger; and push with that the crookedness of the fracture inward with facility from the outward sides, with your other hand from the inward side of the bone; with that decently split that.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated in two, then let the extension of its two parts be according to rightness so that the equality of it is possible.
And if tooth concussion to disruption occurs, then strengthen those of which you expect they will break with thread of gold or silver or gut; and then place on the broken jaw a poultice, and then place on that doubled cloth, and place on the cloth great star-shaped holes decently made, or a sole ingot at its length; and then ligate that above according to how you are equipped for its application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to have rest and tranquillity, and give him wine to drink.
And if you think that the figure is already altered in some way, then cover and loosen it on the third day; and then rectify what is already altered of it and plaster that with sesame flour, with yolk of egg, or with farina of sesame, after you have removed its poultice, and place on the plaster wool of linen.
And then when that plaster and bandage are on it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave that, so that it is not loosened until it is healed and the fracture strengthened; for many times this fracture is tightened in three weeks.
And if another cautery wound occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its swelling so that it is healed, and let the cautery wound be cleaned.
If the fracture is compound, then consider whether the bone is separated or shattered. Then you must skillfully move those shards with what is convenient in the removal of those [shards] with instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then widen it with phlebotomy according to the amount of your need.
Then, when you have moved those shards, and none of them remain exposed, then close the opening of the wound if it is wide; but if not, place one of the appropriate ointments over it, which will help it to heal.
Chapter 5:
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is dislocated.
Many times the furcola of the front part is broken due to a blow from a muscle, and that fracture happens in one of three ways:
Because it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without shattering; and its restoration is easier; or it happens that shards occur in it, and its restoration is difficult; or the fracture is compound.
And the operation on it, when the fracture is compound, is that you have two assistants, so that one holds the joint and follows the broken collarbone, and the other pulls the neck towards that side.
Then, attach the fracture with threads so that it is made according to its shape, and there is not a little prominence or concavity.
And if you need more extension, then place a cord made of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit, and let its size be according to the amount of your need; and extend and raise the collarbone, and hold the cord with your hand so that it ties the fracture according to its shape.
And if you cannot bring the end of the collarbone to the sides outside, because it has been reached by the shoulder, then let the patient be supine on his neck, and place a cushion under his armpit, half in size.
And the assistants press his armpit down to the sides below so that the collarbone bone is elevated higher than the shoulder above, and then you straighten the fracture and attach it with threads.
But if you feel shards of the collarbone and they are exposed, then cut there and extract those shards with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you invent an incision on it with one of the instruments which you have prepared for that, after having placed under the collarbone the instrument which serves the ciphac; and the instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is the shape of it:
And it is like a spoon of which this is the concavity; and its breadth should be according to your need for it, according to the size of the bone or the thinness of it.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for you in the operation; and let it have two ends thus divided, and one end be broad and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound which you have cut when you extracted the shard is broad, and it is sure that there will be an abscess, then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the opening is small, or you fear an abscess, then fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the amount of the split in the wound.
And if an abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in hot vinegar and oil, and place it over that.
And altogether, that the stake is not exposed over the broken bone; and so that you see the patient every day, that when the ligament softens or you see the stake already exposed, then you straighten that and strengthen the binding.
And if the patient is broken in two or three parts, then make a stopa over which you have spread powders doubled with rose vinegar, and place that over the collarbone, and then attach it with threads.
And do not loose the ligament unless an abscess occurs in the place or pus, until 12 days have passed.
And renew the ointment if you have seen its need for that, and tighten in the binding and restore that and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the collarbone has clotted.
And it is tightened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and in some people it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a broken shoulder.
The spatula is broken in its middle and not at its edges.
And when the bone is broken — and it is broken in its middle —  and it is not known except by touch, then according to how the fracture appears, you shall prepare it and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way that is possible for you.
Then place on the site powdered white alum and a stopper made of lion's tooth; then place on it powdered linen folded twice; then place over it a thin piece of slate according to the quantity of the spatula, or a little more.
But if the slate is hollow in one of the places of the spatula, then fill that hollowness with lion's tooth and then raise the slate evenly.
Then tightly bind it above with a long bandage with appropriate pressure, and firm the slate so that it does not slip from its place.
And consider the healing every day; and when it is soft in any place, bind it again and tighten it and equalize the slate if it has slipped from its place.
And keep the patient on his side.
And the spatula is restored in 20 days or in 25. [The number here varies depending on the manuscript].
And therefore when this number is complete, then you shall dissolve the bandage and know for sure that this is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you see that the place of the bone is swollen and you put under it a cord, then you shall split it over it and push it and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the collarbone with pus cauterization if it occurs here.
Chapter 7. [The following chapter begins a new subject].
Of the restoration of a broken foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in its middle and this is evident there, but its ends are most often crushed and comminuted.
And of its accidents when the middle of it is broken there is a decline to the ends within and pain occurs in it, and difficulty in walking and coughing and great vomiting blood, and it is evident in it hollowness in the broken bone and is not concealed by sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient be laid on his back and a cushion placed between his shoulders, and pressure applied to the muscles of him, and the ribs adjusted with the hands of two people, and subtle in the operation of it and in the equalization according to every way that is possible for this and what is convenient for this, so that the figure of the bone returns according to how it was.
Then place on it plaster and wool and place on that subtle slate of willow or of holly, or something like them in levitation, after you have wrapped that in linen, and subtle the binding over the broken bone so that it does not slip; and go around that with bandage according to roundness to the back sometimes, and tighten that with appropriate pressure; and consider the bandage at every hour and when it is soft, tighten that.
And if necessity demands the removal of that you must cut away the callus or growth, or pain, or pus that occurs in the site, then you should cut and dissolve that and eradicate the plaster and correct what has occurred by way of healing of it; and reduce the plaster, if you see this is necessary for the face; and tighten on him the binding so that it may be healed.
Chapter 8. [The following chapter begins a new subject].
Of the restoration of ribs when they are cracked.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur except in large places that go along the back; and from their ends at the front, pain does not occur to them unless they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not concealed by sensation because of the inquiry of it with fingers.
And its restoration is to be done by gripping the fracture with fingers according to the possible way, so that it may be swallowed according to how it was, and then plaster and tighten on him the broken bone if it demands this.
And if the fracture of ribs inclines to the ends within, then there occurs to the patient vehement pain and stabbing, such as that which occurs to him who has wept, and because of that the bone puts the veilment; and occurs in it difficulty of breathing and coughing and vomiting of much blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And already the ants are made in it with moving things.
And from them there is this advice: that you place foods for the patient that generate inflammation and fullness so that the belly may be swollen and extended, and the fracture be driven to the outward ends.
But we abstain from it so that it not be cause to generate pus cauterization, unless it occurs; and if it does occur, then it already helps.
And some of them say that the wind should be applied over it and that it suck with strength; and is like a ratiocination, but nevertheless is feared that the wind sends the superfluities to the site by weakness.
And some of them say: that the site be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and that powdered linseed be placed between the ribs so that it may be filled, so that the binding be wrapped around it according to roundness; and then let the patient be cured with a poultice of food and medicine.
And if it causes violent constriction of the patient who does not suffer, and the bone puts the veil over him with piercing pain, we should fear for the patient; then let us split open the site, uncover from the cracked rib, and then place on it instrument that conserves the cephalic, the memory of which is preceded, and that we pierce the bone with facility, and extract that.
And then let us adjust the two lips of the wound if it is large with cauterization and cure that with plasters so that it may be healed.
And if pus occurs within, then you should cut and pour powdered linseed in warm oil over the site, and that the patient be cured of that by what it does to the pustule within.
And let him be laid on his side on which he slept so that he may be healed.
Chapter 9. [The following chapter begins a new subject].
Of the restoration of fracture of the spine of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes the bones of the neck are fractured, and they are evident in their compression, and likewise the vertebrae of the back. And when this occurs in any of them and you want to know if it will be healed or not, then consider, for if you see the fingers are already soft and dead and do not move those nor extend them and when they constrict them with your fingers or prick them with an awl and do not feel pain in them, then know that it is not healed, and in the midst of their dispositions is deadly.
And if you constrict them and they feel pain in their piercing, then know that the neck of the bone is saved and that the patient will heal by its cure.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck like this and want to know if it will be healed or not, then consider the feet of him.
Then if you see that they are already soft and pain occurs in them as it did in the fingers, and then when he lies on his back, feels hollowness and egestion according to his will, and sometimes is resupinated over his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then know that it is deadly, and therefore do not trouble yourself with its cure.
But if these causes do not arise, the cause is better and closer by.
And the cure for it is that you pierce into the swelling of the boil with a cautery needle that you heat over a warm charcoal fire, or with a needle heated in oil; place this on the swelling several times a day until the boil is healed.
And then apply one of the comforting and soothing plasters, and apply a bandage over it; and order the patient to take rest and tranquility, and not to sleep on the side that causes pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that the bone is separated from the socket or any other cause, then you must cut on the tendon and tie up the bone; and then sew together the two lips of the wound, if it is large; and then treat the wound with a poultice of garlic, until it is healed.
But if the back of the elbow is broken, and it is the end of the bone that is broken, then you must insert the tip of your left hand into the hole, and bite the broken bone with your other hand as hard as you can by closing your fist; and then apply a plaster and bandage on it, if necessary, and then bind it.
And if you feel broken bones in it, then you must cut on it and tie up the bone, and treat the wound with the cure that has been described until it is healed.
Chapter 10:
On the restoration of width.
The bones of the pelvis are often broken, and if they are broken, their fracture is nothing but a separation at their ends, and they are ingrown in length and decline towards the inner parts; and the patient feels pain in the place and piercing, and the leg is numbed when it is fractured.
And its restoration is that you extend your hand over it so that it is above the fracture, in whatever way that is; and then place your other hand under the fractured bone, and press hard on it towards the first hand.
And if the fracture is only at its end, then you bite it according to how it appears to you by closing your fist until it is reassembled into its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in its length or inclines towards the inner parts, then you make the patient lie on his stomach so that you can restore the fracture. And when you have reassembled it, apply a plaster on it; and then place a bandage made of wood or rope over it, and tie it tightly so that you don't fear the puncturing of the fracture or the removal of the bandage.
And fill the cavity with something that fills it so that it takes on the tightness according to its equality; and order the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if there is a boil, then cease from extending it and restoring it until the boil is healed; and then return to its restoration, and bind it thus.
And if they are broken or crushed, then you treat them.
Chapter 11:
On the restoration of the fracture of the thigh.
The adductor is what is between the hip and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a wooden board of moderate thickness according to this shape:
And let there be two ties at both ends, and then it is suspended from the elevated place.
And let the patient walk on crutches; and then let him push out his broken arm over the board so that it reaches the tip of the board in the place to which the curve of the board is attached.
And then let something heavy be suspended over it, or the servant extend it towards the inner parts; and then the doctor press the fracture with his hands, until the fracture returns as it should.
And the other way is that the patient lies on his neck and suspends his arm from the collar of his neck with a rope; and then command two servants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture with his hands, and the other holds the lower part of it; and each of them extends it towards its side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you press on the fracture with your hands and also with another hand, and each of the two servants extends it towards its side.
And if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then you must place half of the bandage on the tip and the other half below the fracture above the hip.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the hip, then you must place the bandage on that place and over the hip.
Bite it with ease and without pain, and then when it is reassembled according to how it should be, bind it if there is no boil.
But if there is a boil, then loosen the bandage on the seventh day, and apply wool soaked in wine vinegar and oil over the boil until it rests, and then bind it.
And the way to bind it is that you apply a plaster on the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then lift up his arm over the adductor, and let him hold his hand open over his muscle.
And place the cloth and bind it over the adductor so that the arm is in its place, unless something prohibits it from the broken bone.
And then use bandages.
And it is that you place over the fracture the bandage that is wider and stronger than the other bandages; and place between each bandage the width of a finger, and let the length of the bandages be according to the fracture with an addition of three digits of the whole hand.
And then bind over the bandages with the tightness that was said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your binding over the place of the fracture be very tight. And when the fracture is longer, then let the binding be lesser.
And thus if you see the placement of the bandages and the binding as we have said in the time in which you restore the member to its position, then you do it.
And if you fear a boil, then you loosen the bandages and the binding on the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the bandage in all parts for three days so that it does not come to the place of pressure or swelling; or food is prohibited from coming to the member due to disposition of excess of binding; and then rectify all that according to what we have said.
But if you are sure that nothing happens, then do not loosen the ligature except after three days or more.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his hands be over his stomach.
And place over the adductor a poultice of wool wrapped in, and consider throughout the night or day that it is not loosen or dissolved, or that the figure of the broken member is softened, then rectify all with your skill.
And let the patient eat according to the order that we have previously mentioned, so that the food is subtle in the beginning so that it is started binding; and then let it become coarse for him.
The custom of the adductor and of his bed is that they are bound for 40 days, and then let them be loosen and let him take a bath and be anointed with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is fresh, then you must not remove that ligament and the splinters within one or two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is made up of two bones which are called two beams; one of which is small and is that which lies under the elbow-joint, and the other is large and is that which lies under the small one from the part further down.
And often only the larger beam is broken, or the smaller one, and often they are both splintered.
And when the smaller beam is broken above, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is even easier.
And when the beam is broken below its fracture is bad and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when two bones are splintered at the same time.
Therefore, when the broken bone is the smaller beam above, then it is appropriate for the doctor, in performing its restoration, to apply slight extension with ease so that it may be enswathed.
But if the larger beam is what is splintered, then one must ensure that there is more violent extension.
And if both beams are splintered at the same time, then you must apply motion with the greatest strength.
And you must apply the hand-position during its restoration and extension over a cushion, with the palm of the hand towards the upper parts, further out than all the others, and with the auricular finger further down than all the others, and with the patient reclining on his back over a raised pillow.
And if the fracture is of the shoulder-joint, then you must bring it to rest and apply great extension over a raised pillow.
Then one minister should extend the arm from below with his hand or with a ligament, and another minister should similarly extend it above.
And the doctor should enswathe the bone in such a way that he reduces it according to the best possible figure.
And if there are gaps in the bone, then you must reduce all of the pieces into their location with your skill.
And if it appears to you that there is a gap which puts the tendon in danger and it does not seem to you hopeful regarding its restoration, then you should stop at that place and treat it according to the manner we have described above.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already written about that topic in a previous chapter, and so you should take its cure from there.
And if pustules appear at the beginning of your restoration, then you should take a cloth with ointment made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be softer between being thin and firm, and tightly place the cloth over that pustule with gentle pressure so that the pustule yields; and then remove the cerot and apply plaster prepared from ground mullet with egg yolk, and then apply splinters; and let the splinter which is placed over that fracture be the firmer and thicker.
And know that the number of splinters of the arm is seen in the greater dispositions, or whether the fracture is in one beam or in both beams at once.
And then apply your extension over the location of the fracture more strongly and violently, and when you extend it to the upper parts of the fracture or to its lower parts, apply the extension a little softer according to how it was preceded in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture should be soft moist cloths and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is tightly bound should be of the appropriate thickness between being thin and thick as we have recommended.
And consider the member and the ligament after several days. And if some cause occurs by which correction is necessary, as when it happens to the member, then you should bathe the member in warm water so that that pain ceases.
And let the member not be constricted while resting, and then return the extension.
And if the stricture is already softened and the bones are already relaxed and resembling those [bones], then rectify all of that with your virtue.
And consider more if the nutrition is prohibited from coming to the member by superfluity of stricture, then you must soften that a little and leave it for several days so that the nutrition can run to it, and then strengthen it.
But if no cause happens to the patient from those we have mentioned, then you must not dissolve it within about 20 days or so.
And let the hand of the patient be suspended by your virtue of movement, and make that one sleep on their own back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or quickly it is restored in 28 days, and all of that is according to the disposition of the complications and the dispositions of virtue.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thumb and fingers.
Know that in the palm of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers there is a great tendency to fracture, and not much happens to them except for a few times.
And when fracture or sprain happens to the hand, then you must make the patient lean forward and lie down according to equality, and then place his hand over that extended.
And let the minister extend the broken bones and the doctor heal those until they are united with good conjunction.
And then you must place plaster and bandages if pustule does not happen cauterize; and then place a splint over it according to the quantity of the place, and you have already wrapped that in linen cloth.

But if the fracture is at the parts outside, then you must place over it splint and another splint of the part below in the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then pause the stricture, as how the hand is rolled to the ratchet between the fingers with ligaments.

Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the thigh.
The bone of the thigh many times is broken and it is declared to the feeling according to how it is converted to the party in front and to the upper pustule.
And its restoration is done so that the ligament is strengthened over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture, and the patient lies on his belly and face down.
And let the minister extend all the ligament to its party according to quality when the fracture is in the middle place of the bone.
But if it is near to the root of the thigh, then let the ligament be near to the armpit at the party of the little finger, so that the extension box in itself, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the break is near to the armpit, then let the ligament be made near to the armpit, and let the extension be at the parties below.
And let the doctor heal the bone with both hands until it reduces that according to the natural resemblance and the bone is joined by good conjunction; then you must place plaster and bandages if pustule does not happen to the member. But if pustule happens to it, then you must leave that for several days until the pustule cauterizes, and then return to your cure.
The bandage of it must be done so: cover over the fracture a hard bandage, and fold that two or three times, and absorb from it superfluity; and then wrap the leg so that it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.

And then fill the emptiness between the leg and the thigh with soft cloths, so that the stricture is coagulated.

And then extend two ends of the thread at the parties below until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and strengthen both ends of the splints on the other side so that the ligament is not relaxed from its place.
And then leave the bandage over that, where it is so that no pustule happens to the member or cauterize or inflammation and resembling those; and if any cause from those happens, then you know of its dissolution, and rectify all that happens to it totally, according to how we said many times.
But if in the bone is bone of the bone which you put, then you heal that according to what it is possible for you; and if not, leave it and heal that, and cure with that from which the account precedes so that it heals.
And some times this fracture of the thigh is strengthened another with which they are tightened to it, as we said in the helper and in the arms.

Chapter 15.

Chapter .xvi.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg: and when there is a fracture of the bone great or small, with whatever you like, place the patient upon the manes of a horse or ox or camel or donkey, and let the head be in the middle between two manes, and thus he shall be borne towards the rim of the sun until he is healed.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick and is named by the name of the leg; and the other is thin and is named the shank.
And it happens to both kinds of fractures what happens to the two bones of the arm, and by that is made the restoration of it just as the restoration of the arm similarly, and the operation of it is one.
And if both bones are broken together, then the leg is turned into all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, then the leg is turned to the rear.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it turns that way below, and it is of those causes which are not to you unknown.
Then administer extension and equation and ligament of the stars according to that similarly.
But indeed, if the fracture of the leg is loose having many splinters, then let the extension be less and more distant, and draw it with your virtue.
And in the leg there is some cause of operation on the arm, and it is when you equalize the stars and that you complete all your operation, then you shall take weights of wood of pine which is placed below the causes which are placed in soles, and are made splinters of white of palm; you shall attach those to which is a small thickness, and it shall not be of the fingerless, and the length of those according to the length of the leg, and of the rear within to the most distant.
Then wrap around each one in two wrappings according to the length of it, and place one over the fracture of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and they shall be within the most distant location of the foot.
Then tie the two stars in three locations of two extremes and in the middle location, for with this string the leg is prohibited from tilting to the right side and left side, and it stands in good position.
And a brace of wood has been administered according to the length of the leg; and it is placed upon it so that it conserves that from movement.
And indeed, it is better that you do that in fracture, and when with that is plague properly.
And indeed, consider the leg in each day and study it with that for the last study, and keep it from ulcer and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any of those causes happen, then treat it with what is appropriate until it is healed.
Chapter .xvii.
Of the fracture of the leg and of the shank and of the bones of the foot and of the toes.
To the talo no happens fracture of all; but to the bones of the foot happen fracture some times; and to the toes of the foot rarely happens fracture, and it does not happen to them except in the months of disposition.
And if to the bones of the foot happens fracture and those bones are made to be elevated upon each other, then let the patient put his foot upon the earth prepared thus:
Then place one foot over what is elevated of those bones, and indeed press and equalize those within that they turn in the locations.
Then apply plaster and stop it above, and place below the belly of the foot a small table to which are two pegs planted.
Then tighten that with the foot with a descending string, more than you have wrapped it with cloths and with other causes of which you need.
And when to it have passed three days or four, then you unbind the string, for you shall find the bones equalized or broken or dislocated, and the operation in those is thus with what is known.
But if any toes are broken, then restore those and equalize them just as you and instructed in the restoration of the toes of the hand.
