And it is convenient to you that you do not forget that you do not consider all the causes which are to you told in the other fractures of the accidents which we have said; and treat every accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter .xviii.
Of the fracture of the heel and of the bone of the instep and of the staff of man.
When the heel is broken, then let it be made to bend in a shoe, and indeed let it be tilted to the side of two little, and let it be held behind.
And indeed the midwife fills the heel with cotton, and it is made in the heel thus as sperry; and indeed let the instep be bound, and elevate both little by little, for all that is bent until it is before the fracture thus with sperry and the broken bone returns.
Then place powder upon the instep and when he wants to urinate let him moisten the heel with facility and indeed urine, and reduce it and return that according to the way according to which you measured it at the beginning, and indeed return to his first powder.
And do thus for seven days or environ those, for that is restored.
And if you want, take a woolen bag and tighten over the hole of it a tube of reed; and insert the whole bag in the heel, and indeed blow into the tube with strength until the bag is inflated within the heel, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with cotton and stay for some days according to that we have instructed, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the instep of man or woman is broken, then administer in the restoration and piercing of it what we have instructed in the bone of the hip.
And what is straight is not hidden in these close fractures and that hardly comes to mind, indeed listen best to my free speech; for that fracture indicates to you in the greatest dispositions way straighter in the restoration and in the string of it, and therefore understand well.
But when the penis of a man is broken or fractured, take a gourd of walnut wood and insert the penis into it; then wrap a cloth around him, and have him wrapped up, and leave him for three days or so, and he will be healed.
Chapter XIX.
Of the restoration of fractures in bones when they are infected.
It is convenient for the person to whom a fracture happens to have the plague, and especially if the bone is large as it is in the thigh or the adjutorium, or similar to that, which you treat with cauterization and bleeding as soon as the conditions of cauterization indicate to you, as we have said above.
And if the plague flows with blood, then you should take care to stop it in such a way that you powder over it powdered alder bark, unless another cause is present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on the same day, and do not prolong it, if an abscess does not occur.
But if an abscess occurs, then you should postpone the restoration of it until the ninth day, while the abscess subsides, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause ill effects.
But if the broken bone protrudes above the skin, then take care to reduce and draw it back with your hands with ease and with little extension.
But if extension is not ready and the position of it with your hands, then you should reduce that with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length is seven digits or eight and its breadth is according to the quantity of the plague; and it is necessary for the doctor to take one or two of these, according to the quantity needed in the cure in every kind of fracture.
And it should be round and not have much thickness in it so that it is not bent under the pressure upon it during the operation.
And its upper part should be thick and its middle part should be thin and into the edges.
And this is the shape of it:
And in Greek it is named “labro volenz atelarem”, and it is a little spatula.
For it is necessary that the end of it be placed elevated upon the end of the prominent bone.
And push it into a groove until it returns to its position and let it be drawn into any position.
And then try to draw the ends of the fracture together; and if the end of the broken bone is thin and does not take in that instrument well, then you should insert the end of that bone so that the instrument can do its work.
But if you cannot reduce the bone at all with what we have recommended, then insert that into whatever is convenient for it of the chisels which we have mentioned; or saw that with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then smooth that which protrudes in the bone with roughness and tiny scars.
And when the bone is reduced and especially when the reduction gives the patient great pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and therefore do what you confer to the patient great benefit.
When you have completed your restoration which you do to the bone, then immerse cloth in black vinegar and especially if it is summer, and do not put ointment or any other cause on the plague in which there is oil, so that you will not cause rot and corrosion on it.
And then administer stars in the hour of your completion of the restoration of the bone, and leave the plague uncovered so that you insert the thread with forceps and use bandages according to the quantity of the plague.
And beware that you do not strain the plague with the fracture, for many times they have done this the foolish doctors, and have caused death or corrosive putrefaction or putrescence on their patients, and it is a sickness. And if your grip is loose, soft, different from the grip of the other fractures.
And if the plague is large or evil, and you fear some ill effects on that which we have recommended, and it hurts in the place, then do not put stars over that but put on that bandages of hard cloth in place of stars and bind it with those.
And when one or two days have passed and the plague has begun to become heated with inflammation, then take off the cloth which you had put over that with oil of vitriol, and then apply warm poultices, as from our custom is that we medicate plagues, thus with tetrafarmacon poultice and similarly.
And it seems good to you that you loosen the bandage and consider the plague every day morning and evening until it is consolidated and closed, and let the member be prepared in such a way that the putrefaction runs from it to the ends below with ease.
But if days pass on the plague and it is not consolidated or the inflammation is not removed from it, then know that there are hidden things within that which cause you to tent the plague. And from those things which are separated and extracted, those that when they are not separated cause the member to be in pain, then pierce them according to what you can do it possible.
And if the plague becomes suppurating or ulcerous, it is putrefying or another kind of corruption and putrefaction occurs, then do not pause over either one of them what benefits him in the cure of which there is a precedent in his chapter.
And about that in which there is the middle term and you make your own judgment: when a bone is broken, protrude over the member like the bone of the thigh or the adjutorium and similarly to those of the large members, and do not presume attraction on it, nor its extraction for many times death occurs from this; have left that as it is so that putrefaction occurs, for it falls very firm by itself after twenty days or thirty. And then cure the plague if you see it, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Of the cure for knots which happen in the path of a fracture.
Knots often happen in the path of healing from a fracture, especially near the joints, and it changes the shape of the limb; and it strongly prohibits the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, consider if the knot is recent and administer to it medicines which have a styptic effect like aloe, and aloes, and myrrh, and similar things; and take some or all of them and mix them with white vinegar, or with sour wine, or with sharp wine, and apply that to the knot on a piece of cloth, and constrict that around it with a good constriction.
And let the constriction not be loosened for several days; and then loosen it and repeat this, while the knot is being reduced; or place a sheet of lead over it, leaning downward, because lead has the property of reducing anything that is swollen in the limbs.
But if the knot is already converted to stone and it is constricted and necessity demands cutting out that knotted part completely, then split that from its most upper part, and cut away the excess and prominent part, or pierce it with some sharp resoring instrument until it is reduced; and cure that as it heals.
Chapter 21.
Of the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and the limb underneath it is subtle and fragile, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and the limb underneath it is subtle and fragile, that is not done but for several causes: one of which is too much solutio of the ligament and of its ligature not according to how it should be; or by excess of constriction of the ligaments so that nutrition is prohibited from running to the limb; or by too much superfluous embrocation; or by superfluous movement not at the right time; or by lack of blood in the patient's body and by weakness of him.
And the cure for this is to give the patient food and make his heart tender so that blood is multiplied in him; and administer a bath; and apply heat and lard on it.
And then place a plaster over the limb so that the plaster brings it much nutrition, or continue embrocation of it with warm water until the nutrition runs and returns to its natural shape.
Chapter 22.
Of the cure of broken bones when they are restored crookedly and prohibit their operation from that which is natural to them.
When it happens to the limb already restored that its healing is crooked and prominent at the broken bone and knotted, and it is fed by form of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not prohibited from its natural function; yet do not believe the word of him who examines that limb that it is newly broken.
But there have already been several foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land; and this operation is very reproachful, presenting great danger, the sign of which is deadly.
But if the claudication and knot are recent, then let them be embrocated with warm water in which soft herbs are steeped, like leaves of holly and roots of it, and mallows, and similar things to those medicines that are named, brother consolidatio; or let it be plastered with a plaster made of mucilage of roots of holly and let it be bound with linen rags or with cotton wool, and let it be plastered with that; or let them be bound with fat figures and bound with dove droppings, and similar things to those medicines.
And some times it is dissolved by constant gentle friction which should be done with the hands moistened, and movement of the limb in every direction at all hours should be administered.
But if the crookedness has already gone forward and is made strong and converted into stone and necessity demands curing it with iron, then let the pus be removed from above and the continuity of the bone be dissolved, and that which superfluously knots the muscle at that bone be cut by subtle incisers, and facility be administered in that by solicitude and study.
Chapter 23.
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is dislocation of one of the joints of its place, so that it is prohibited from movement, and changes the shape of the limb, and brings great pains and injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when dislocation happens, then let it be forced to be reduced immediately, and do not delay its reduction, because if it is delayed, the joint will become inflamed, and very difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And for that reason let it not be bent or stretched during the inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and pains in them.
But when that happens, let you care for the phlebothimia of the patient, and then let it be left until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be embrocated with warm water and oil, and then reduced with facility.
And every limb should be cured by that of which the remembrance will come to its place.
And you have ordered the chapters of dislocation according to that which precedes in fracture, from the highest part of the body to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
Of the cure for dislocation of the lower jaw.
Both mandibles are dislocated if not tart; and the dislocation of both is done according to one of two ways: that they are swollen from their place by little motion, and become soft a bit; or are dislocated by entire dislocation and complete, so that they are swollen at the joint where saliva runs from the patient and he cannot hold that nor close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped in its speech.
But when the dislocation of it is little, then in the major dispositions it returns by itself for little cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and complete, then let the reduction of it be administered gently, and not be delayed at all.
And let the minister hold the patient's head, and let the doctor insert his finger of one hand in the root of the dislocation of the mandible within the mouth of him if the dislocation is on one side; or that he inserts both fingers together if the dislocation is on two sides, and the other fingers be out with which he hugs; and command the patient to cease his mandibles and release those from every part; and let the doctor hug the mandible and press the mandible so that it returns to its place.
And if the reduction of it is difficult, and especially if both mandibles are dislocated together, then administer embrocation with warm water and oil, so that the reduction of both be made gently and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And then when they return and are hugged, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not made soft, then place over them powders of cloth with cerot, and let it be made of wax and rosin rubbed; and then let it be bound by facility with mild ligatures.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be between two pillows, so that he does not turn to the right nor to the left; and do not force him to open for any cause; having his food be lean meat, so that when the pains are reduced and the mandibles are swollen, then let him open what he will.
And let this be administered with ease, and do not labor over the opening of the mouth because of eating, drinking, and speech, while the jaw is numbed and healed.
And if it is difficult to reduce both mandibles when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then often sudden fevers occur, as well as continuous vomiting, and the belly of the patient quickly becomes swollen, and he vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is lethal; many times the person upon whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter 25
On the reduction of a dislocation of the collarbone and of the end of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated on the inside by its connection to the feet; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outer end, and this is indicated by sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. Then press the spot hard with your palm, for it will return.
And apply plaster and powder to that spot, and strengthen it.
But the end of that which is connected to the collarbone and continued from it is not dislocated at any time; therefore it must be reduced and bound as we have said, and as your tools allow.
And apply plaster and powder and a binding to that spot, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And by this treatment the end of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26
On the reduction of a dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated at the end of the tendon at the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this occurs seldom; it is not dislocated at the sides by the shoulder, nor at the front by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and exits out at the lower parts by the tendon, and mostly in those of whom there is little flesh, it is dislocated with ease; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it exits out with difficulty.
And quickly occurs in some men by concussion or blow, and the muscle of them is apoplectic from apoplexy, and it is cured because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore know this while you are in supination of it, and then proceed in the cure of it.
And the dislocation of it is known when it is at the lower parts by the tendon; so that you may distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the sound muscle, for you will find between them manifest difference; and you will know it in the head of the muscle concave and below the tendon because of touch, and the patient cannot reduce his hand to his ear on that side, nor move it at all.
If it is dislocated behind or inside, and you feel touch clearly and it is not concealed.
And this dislocation has an easier reduction when fresh and the patient is young.
And its reduction is that the minister lifts the arm of him up above; and then place your fingers below the tendon of him, and elevate the joint with strength towards its place, and the minister lifts the arm of him, and extends it upwards; and then place that below because it will return easily.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and only after the dislocation occurred, then let the patient be bathed in warm water and be administered an embrocation that softens and lubricates as if his thigh were razor-cut, with fenugreek, and honey in water, and be administered.
And then the patient is supinated on his back and a pillow is placed below the tendon of him, and he awaits in elevation and compression, and the doctor places his heel over the joint, and lifts the head of the muscle with strength, and pulls the hand of the patient so that it is not pulled down to the lower parts; and another minister holds the head of the patient so that it is not pulled down, for then that returns at once to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this manner: and have a man present who is taller than the patient, and let him stand on that side, and insert his muscle below the tendon of the patient and lift the tendon of him up above while the patient is suspended in air; and another minister pulls the hand of the patient from the lower parts of his belly. But if the patient is light, then know that with him who for another cause is suspended, for gravity brings about the return of the dislocation at once.
And it is reduced likewise according to another manner: and let a long post be placed in the ground, the head of which is round in figure, like mortar, that is not thick nor thin; and then let the patient be placed below, and let sheets of linen be placed over the head of the post, and the patient stand according to the length of the post, and then let his arm be extended from the lower parts on the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place with swiftness.
And if its reduction is difficult for all the causes we have said, then use this cure: and take a post whose quantity is in the length of two cubits, and the breadth of it is of four digits, and the thickness of it is removed in the length of the tendon according to this form:
And then over the round head are tied linen sheets so that the patient does not slide down.
And then the tendon of the patient is placed below, and his arm or hand is extended over the post to the lower parts; and the post is tied over the helper, and the arm and the end of the hand.
Then the arm is placed over a rung of a ladder according to its breadth, and the hand is extended to the lower parts; and the other body is suspended from the other side, for the joint enters at once.
And when the instrument of it is completed according to whatever manner is possible for you, and then know that you place below the tendon a sponge of wool of equal quantity; and then place plaster prepared from powder of moly with honey over all the muscle, as it is rolled above; and then bind it below the tendon with binding decently; and the bandage is rolled over the plaster, and his hand is attached to his neck, and he is let loose, and his hand is not pulled for seven days.
And know that the food of the patient be lessened while the member is comforted, for it is easier for the cure of him.
And then food is given more freely, and exercise is added; and if it is firm and not soft, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times by moisture that occurs in him or for another cause, then know that in that be administered cautery with three punctures, according as it is said before in the chapter of cautery.
And if all of this is done, and then after seven days the bandage and binding are removed from him, and the joint is not firm, and it is cut and catched, and he cannot lift that arm to the upper parts, know that the nerves of him which are in the head of the muscle are already incised or extended or soft, and know that the joint never returns to its place.
From the dislocation of that which is done from behind the feet and the breasts or the parts, the reduction is done by impulse and extension with the hands, while returning.
And be administered in that other constriction, and cure that one until it is healed.
And if the healing of the body part happens slowly and with difficulty in its movement, then the patient should take many warm baths until that heaviness is relieved and the body part returns to its original state.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of dislocation of the neck.
Know that the neck joint is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated from all sides, and especially from the front to the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because when you move the neck joint or touch it according to how it is dislocated, this will be made clear to you, for the joint is hollow and cannot bend the arm, nor can it reach with that hand.
And when you see this, and reduce the dislocation immediately before it comes to an abscess, for if it comes to an abscess, its reduction is difficult, and often it will not be healed at all; and especially when it is on the back side, for it is worse than all other kinds of dislocations, and more painful, and many times results in death.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is for the hand to be extended with two hands, and for the arm to be expanded, and for the doctor's hands to be on the neck and below it, and for him to push the joint with his finger tips, or with the edge of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is on the front side, then it returns as he bends his hand with his own hand, while he strikes the neck with the edge of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer a violent extension and a very strong push.
And two ministers should extend the arm and hold that joint so that it is not wrenched because of the extension.
Then the arm is wrapped around with a long piece of folded cloth or bandage.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should smear it with some oil so that it may be caused by those things which help in lubricating the joint with ease; and then push the joint with a violent impulse until it returns.
And then its reduction should be made over that plaster in which it has been stiffened and dried with white of egg, and tightened with a descending bandage, and the arm suspended from the neck, and left for several days.
And then it should be unwrapped, and if the joint is formed in its place, then you should unravel the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not stronger, then you should repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is tightened, and then unravel it.
But if it happens to him that the heaviness remains after the reduction of the joint, and its disposition is for movement, then administer moistening in a bath and subtle friction, and compression, until he is relieved; or pause on the joint, olive oil so it is a fat of quick action; and then bandage it, and leave it for the day and night. And then wash that one and put him in a bath; and when he has bathed, then you should rub the joint with tempered friction, and then repeat on that olive oil, so it is of quick action, once, and the second, and the third. Corruption has already come upon the inner part of the brain, as it were a membrane.
And if you wish, place over that cow's udder which is wet and warmed with butter and stretched over it; and do this several times, for this is wet and reduces to its disposition.
Chapter 28.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow joint, which is between the arm and the hand.
The elbow of the hand sometimes becomes dislocated, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints, except when it happens in the hour in which it is displaced, before the place becomes abscessed or an abscess comes to it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the joint of the patient's hand on a table and the minister extends his hand from him, and the doctor places his hand or his palm over the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns to its place.
But if it is considered that the palm has already been placed on the belly of the hand, then the patient should place both of his hands on a table because of extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is preceding from the hand, then let the position of the hand of him be intrinically over the table, so that the hand of the doctor falls on that prominence of the joint.
And therefore if it returns immediately, it is good; but if not, then tighten that with plaster, let the abscess pass and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, he will not bear nor be able to endure the reduction of it more than days have passed.
But if the joint becomes twisted according to its tortuosity; and the patient feels no cause, except that his hand is made soft and cannot be tightened for any cause; then know that the nerves are torn or cut, and then there is not pain in him except that it is tightened with cautery near the place of it and very often this indicates no cause.
Then when the elbow is returned, then place over it the plaster which we have recommended, and let it be left and tightened for a few days; and then unravel it and leave it, and the hand of him be exercised.
But if its movement is hindered and some heaviness comes to it, then loosen that with warm water and with friction several times, until he is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes they are dislocated on all sides.
And when some finger is dislocated on the side of the palm or the belly of it, then push the finger and drive out its dislocation with your fingers, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until it is straightened out in that its day.
And when evening comes, loosen that according to that narration, and do not cease to unravel it in the day and exercise with movement, and loosen it at night; and do this for several days until it is tight.
And make it like that if it is dislocated on the belly of the hand, and loosen it to that side and do to it its first operation, until it is healed.
And likewise do to that when it is dislocated on other sides.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of dislocation of the shoulder joints.
When dislocation comes to one of the shoulder joints or the neck, and only dislocated joints remain in their places, then there is no cure for this, for death draws near the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is discharged without their will, and they cannot retain it; and sometimes some of his limbs or both legs or both arms, or one of those, are made soft, or a vertebra in his spine is dislocated, for many times it is dislocated and many times the removal of those [vertebrates] is done in four parts.
Thus that which has been dislocated to the back is called scoliosis.
But its treatment is that when you consider if the scoliosis happens in children, because then there is no cure for it nor any healing at all.
But for that which comes from falling or hitting or similar to those, the Ancients spoke of many ways of treatment with lengthy speech, of which the most beneficial is to return it [the vertebra] with profit; but I have abridged that of which little excuses itself through too much of it, due to my proximity to its intention and exposure, by which I have expounded and formed from diverse instruments of that by which I will show and space out that.
And of those there is no cure except that which happens specifically in the back with what I will account.
And that the patient should be laid down on his back close to a wall, and that he be extended downward by his feet being raised so that they are not bent.
And then a straight post should be placed and fixed in the ground at the end of the bed near the patient's head, and another post under his feet at the other end of the bed.
And the minister should hold the post, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another minister should hold the other second in that form.
And then wrap around the patient's head and down under his armpits a lion-skin firm; and extend the end of the skin to the post which is near the patient's head, and attach that there; and then tighten the other strap over the patient's hips and his knees, and attach those to the other post which is near his feet.
And then each minister should extend the strap to the end of the post, and that the two posts and the straps at their locations where they are attached are not dislocated, but rather are tightened as I have said.
And the doctor should place his palms on the vertebra with strength while he returns it, or that he leans over it on a table, and then that he tightens his feet over the table as he returns it.
But if it does not return by this form, then take a table of which the breadth or length is in the direction of three cubits, and dig into the wall of which we have said that is near the patient a place in which fits the end of the table. And then place the middle of the table over the vertebra, and the doctor should place his feet over the other end, and tighten well while the vertebra is constricted and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open with a turn which is rolled by the hand; and it that you fix in the ground beneath the patient's head two posts, the length of which is one cubit, and fix the patient's head at the end of the bed two posts; and let the length between each post be a palm, and now there are made in each post holes in which runs a rope, and each should be tightly fixed in the ground so that it is not dislocated.
And there should be another round post in which is the turn, and it should be rolled in the two holes of the two posts.
And at its end is a hole in which is formed the post, the length of which is a palm by which it is rolled; and in the other two posts there is similar to that.
And then tighten the straps which you have tightened in the patient' s feet at the hole by which is his head, and the straps which you have tightened in his thighs at the turn by which are his feet.
And then fix each turn and have a minister who twists the turn by his hand, and the doctor similarly the vertebra as we have said.
And after the vertebra is returned and the location is warmed, then put that you apply a plaster of honey with albumen, and then stop it; and then lean over the plaster a board of which the breadth is three digits or surrounding those, and its length is quantity that takes up the location of the scoliosis, and over the end of the vertebra as well.
And then it should be tied with the strap which we have said, and let the patient take subtle food until he is healed.
And if there remains any swelling in the location at the end of the healing, then put that are administered medicines which moisten and smooth with administration of the table for a long time, which we have recounted; and now is administered in that location leaf of lead.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the end of the vertebrae of two, and it is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which now causes so that there should not be made protrusion to him with this medicine, because strongly he will cause death to you.
Chapter .xxxi.
Of the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle do not occur unless dislocation only, and it does not happen to those which happen to other joints of small removal and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated according to four ways, for it is dislocated at the inner parts, or at the outer parts, or at the front parts, or at the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated at the inner parts, and seldom at the front or back parts.
And the sign of its dislocation at the inner parts is that when you straighten the patient's leg with the leg, it is longer and the tendon is more prominent than of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot due to the tongue, and the location which the tongue comes from has become pus-filled here.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens at the outer parts is that its accidents are contrary to those accidents.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens at the front parts is that it expands his leg according to compliment, but it cannot bend without pain that is in the tendon, and if he goes forward and does not bear weight on his foot; and his urine is retained and his tongue is pus-filled, and he bears weight on his heel.
And the sign of that to which dislocation happens at the back parts is that it does not expand the tendon, nor can it bend without that which bends the tongue, and his leg is shorter than the other one, and the tendon is softened, and the head of the hip comes from that location.
But the reduction of the kinds of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and antique, as it is aged by having been given for a long time, then there is not in that a cure at all; and thus there should not be made protrusion to him.
But in the case of a recent dislocation which is one of the four species of dislocation, you must apply to the joint what makes it return and extend it to those on the inside and outside, and especially to that of the right and left, for it quickly returns and does not need another treatment.
But if it does not return in this way, then you must prepare a strong minister and extend the leg of him from the part most below, or with his own hands, or with a bandage which is tied over the leg above the pain; and another minister extends the hand of that one above so that he puts his hands below his ticklish parts; and then tighten with a lion's hide over the root of the thigh, and have another minister hold the end of the hide.
And it should be extended from within to the angle of the breastbone, or from behind to the part of the chest.
And all of these should be extended while the patient is elevated from the ground and his tongue is hanging out.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary in every species to direct the appropriate cure.
In the proper reduction of a dislocation, when it is to the inner parts, the patient should be laid on his side; and then place the bands over the root of the thigh in that spot between the head of the thigh and the part below the tongue; and then extend the band up from the angle of the breastbone to the highest part of the furcula.
And then another strong minister should take the arms of him and press on the large part of his thigh and extend it to the outside by violent extension, for that will return to its place.
And this species is the quickest cure with which this member is reduced compared to the other species.
But if he is unconscious and doesn't obey you when you try this species of cure with both, then you should tie both feet of him together with a strong band, quickly, over both heels and over both toes.
And the length of each of his parts should be the quantity of four digits, and the leg that is dislocated should be extended more than the other by a quantity of two digits.
And then the patient should be suspended over the head of the beam and in the middle; and his length from the ground should be the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to lessen the head of the thigh, and another young man should be suspended with the patient; and the other young man, lessening the thigh with strength, should push, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction when it is dislocated to the outside, he should lie on his back as we have recommended in the part about having coldness; and the band should be tightened over the thigh properly and over the feet of him.
And then two beams should be placed, one under his feet and the other under his head; and then beams that are close to the middle of the beam that is laid over which dampened sheets are wrapped should be placed, so that the patient is not damaged by the beam being between his thighs, for then the extension to the lower parts will not be drawn up.
And then each minister should extend to his part, and the doctor with his hand should guide the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is to the front parts, then the leg should be extended quickly, and according to this narrative it is in this way on the beam; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand over the tongue of the patient, and then he shortens that with his other hand, and with that shortening he extends it to the lower parts to the part of the toe.
And if the dislocation is to the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended to the lower parts, and he is elevated from the ground; but he should be placed on a hard cause, just as we have said when the shoulder is dislocated to the outside, according to what we have said about his placement on two beams; and he lies on his face and the bands are extended, just as we have said, without delay.
And let compression be administered with a board over the place to which the joint extends.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed according to what you have — and the sign of the reduction of him is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend both thighs, and when you see those angles and the patient stretches his thigh and expands with no excuse, then know that the member according to which he has returned — and then you adjust both thighs and pause compression and tighten with a band so that the broad part is not bruised in either of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
And then loosen the band and the paste, and compare one leg to the other.
And then if you see those angles in view, then know that the dislocation is already stopped, and let him leave after.
And if you see any cause of softening, then return and plaster that, and tighten it according to your first tightening, and let him leave after three days.
And then loosen that, and let him delay walking on crutches for several days, until he is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toe.
The toe is dislocated according to three manners, for it is dislocated to the outside, and to the inside, and to the lower parts more than to the back parts; and to the front parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of dislocation of him is that you command the patient to apply his leg against his thigh; then know that the toe is dislocated.
And the restoration of all his manners is that you make the patient lie straight, so that he extends his leg, if he can on that; have him adhere his toe to the face of him, and pause his foot between his feet; then let him adhere his toe with your palms, and hold those between your fingers over the toe of him with strength, and another minister extend his foot while the toe returns to its place.
And the sign of redemption of him is that his leg adheres to his thigh with non-laborious elevation.
And then plaster that and let the band adhere to his thigh, and then have him lie those together with a band for three or four days, and then loosen those.
And do not use the patient for walking for several days until he is healed and comforted.
But if his reduction by you is excused, then administer strong extension with bands of which in the cure of the shoulder preceding narrative was spoken.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toe.
The toe sometimes is bruised by minor removal, and sometimes it is dislocated according to compliment, and its dislocation is made either to the inner parts or to the outside.
And the sign of his dislocation is that you see the talus [ankle bone] swollen proceeding from the part where it is dislocated.
But the cure for his removal is a gentle reduction and that he be extended with ease by hands and wrapped while he returns [to his normal state].
But in the cure of him when he is dislocated according to nature, one should make the patient lie down straight and have a strong and valiant minister from behind hold his hips in the middle of his back in that very spot.
Then with your right hand take the foot from its highest part and with your left hand from the lowest part of the foot in the spot of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and with your left twice, then extend it with your left; and press the bottom of the foot with your right behind the leg as you work.
And do this twice according to what we have counted.
Then press the bottom of the foot to the leg on the third round and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two rounds according to this manner and you see the foot wrapped, it is good; but if not, then repeat the operation upon him until he returns.
But if it is prohibited to you to reduce him with what we have recommended then let the patient lie on his back on the ground and stretch a mat firm on the ground and place a pillow between his thighs and wrap a cloth around him so that he does not turn over.
And let the minister of him have his thighs and let another minister extend the foot with his hands or with an instrument that lies upon the neck of the foot; and let each minister extend against the extension of his party, and let the pillow be extended between the patient's thighs holding it there so that the body is not drawn to the parts below its extension.
And let the doctor wrap the dislocation with his hands and let another minister hold the healthy thigh at the parts below, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore when the dislocation returns and you see the health of its reduction, then pause plaster and stretch that with strong instruments and tie the foot to the leg.
And take care of or guard against the nerves which are behind on the talus that the instrument upon it is not violent and lead that strongly.
Then let him leave it for two or three days and if the instrument is softened then stretch it.
And let him loosen it on the third day or fourth and prohibit the patient from walking for 40 days. But if he is forced to go before that space it is not secure that the dislocation is not dissolved and corrupted upon him and let there be no cure upon him.
And if there happens to him pus by cautery then let you minister to the ceasing of him that in which we preceded our narration, in another place of cure and embrocation, until it is cleaned out.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of cure for dislocation of the toes of the feet.
Let him who is dislocated be wrapped with little extension without difficulty and before it is easier.
But if the dislocation is in any part of the neck of the foot then let him lie down with his foot straight on a flat place upon the earth or table and be standing there as if he were walking.
And let you stand on your feet and place your toe upon that which protrudes from the joints and let you press it with your foot with strength until he returns and see that it is wrapped and there does not appear in the spot any eminence.
And let you place beneath the belly of his foot a table which takes up all the foot and let it have two ends, and let you stretch that with decent and firm strength for three days; then loosen him and let him rest from walking for several days until he is stretched out and his reduction is secure.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the kinds of dislocations which are made by blow or by fracture or by any other impaction.
When that happens for any cause and you are proficient in the cure of that and in the restoration of him, several times succeed death.
And for that not let you presume upon the cure of him but he who is wise in the art, long experienced, merciful and compassionate, tardy, not hasty nor presumptuous; and let him use in the commencement of medicines which yield pus by cautery only, and let him examine the patient to the divine judgement, unless one from whom you expect salvation, from wickedness alleviation, and in which appears some hope. And therefore make haste to do the reduction upon him at the commencement of the cause, before he goes into pus by cautery.
And therefore if the member returns according as we will let you then minister regime which yields pus by cautery and cure the wound with that which suits him of the dry unguents.
And if dislocation is by fracture and impaction happens in the bones separated let you be proficient in their setting and do in that what we have said in the simple maladies of those which are preceded in their places.
And guard and avoid according to your strength that of the instrument in ways of fear according as my prediction is preceded for you are better before your creator, and more permanent to your glory, and healthier to your body will be seen those in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly of which God be beneficent. Amen.
